<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Sector_Germany.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/American_Sector_Germany.png/220px-American_Sector_Germany.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="284" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/American_Sector_Germany.png/330px-American_Sector_Germany.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/American_Sector_Germany.png/440px-American_Sector_Germany.png 2x" data-file-width="464" data-file-height="599" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Sector_Germany.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>General Eisenhower served as military governor of the American zone (highlighted) in <a href="/wiki/Allied-occupied_Germany" title="Allied-occupied Germany">Allied-occupied Germany</a> from May through November 1945</div></div></div>
<p>Following the German unconditional surrender, Eisenhower was appointed military governor of the <a href="/wiki/American_occupation_zone" class="mw-redirect" title="American occupation zone">American occupation zone</a>, located primarily in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Germany" title="Southern Germany">Southern Germany</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Military_Government,_United_States" title="Office of Military Government, United States">headquartered</a> at the <a href="/wiki/IG_Farben_Building" title="IG Farben Building">IG Farben Building</a> in <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_am_Main" class="mw-redirect" title="Frankfurt am Main">Frankfurt am Main</a>. Upon discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_concentration_camps" title="Nazi concentration camps">Nazi concentration camps</a>, he ordered camera crews to document evidence of the atrocities in them for use in the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Trials" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuremberg Trials">Nuremberg Trials</a>. He reclassified German <a href="/wiki/Prisoner_of_war" title="Prisoner of war">prisoners of war</a> (POWs) in U.S. custody as <a href="/wiki/Disarmed_Enemy_Forces" title="Disarmed Enemy Forces">Disarmed Enemy Forces</a> (DEFs), who were no longer subject to the Geneva Convention. Eisenhower followed the orders laid down by the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Joint Chiefs of Staff">Joint Chiefs of Staff</a> (JCS) in directive <a href="/wiki/JCS_1067" class="mw-redirect" title="JCS 1067">JCS 1067</a>, but softened them by bringing in 400,000 tons of food for civilians and allowing more <a href="/wiki/Fraternization" title="Fraternization">fraternization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> In response to the devastation in Germany, including food shortages and an influx of refugees, he arranged distribution of American food and medical equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> His actions reflected the new American attitudes of the German people as Nazi victims not villains, while aggressively purging the ex-Nazis.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:General_Dwight_Eisenhower_in_Warsaw,_1945.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/General_Dwight_Eisenhower_in_Warsaw%2C_1945.jpg/220px-General_Dwight_Eisenhower_in_Warsaw%2C_1945.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/General_Dwight_Eisenhower_in_Warsaw%2C_1945.jpg/330px-General_Dwight_Eisenhower_in_Warsaw%2C_1945.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/General_Dwight_Eisenhower_in_Warsaw%2C_1945.jpg/440px-General_Dwight_Eisenhower_in_Warsaw%2C_1945.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="792" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:General_Dwight_Eisenhower_in_Warsaw,_1945.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>General Eisenhower (left) in Warsaw, Poland, 1945</div></div></div>
<p>In November 1945, Eisenhower returned to Washington to replace Marshall as Chief of Staff of the Army. His main role was rapid demobilization of millions of soldiers, a job that was delayed by lack of shipping. Eisenhower was convinced in 1946 that the Soviet Union did not want war and that friendly relations could be maintained; he strongly supported the new United Nations and favored its involvement in the control of atomic bombs. However, in formulating policies regarding the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic bomb">atomic bomb</a> and relations with the Soviets, <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Truman</a> was guided by the U.S. State Department and ignored Eisenhower and the <a href="/wiki/The_Pentagon" title="The Pentagon">Pentagon</a>. Indeed, Eisenhower had opposed the use of the atomic bomb against the Japanese, writing, "First, the Japanese were ready to surrender and it wasn't necessary to hit them with that awful thing. Second, I hated to see our country be the first to use such a weapon."<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Initially, Eisenhower hoped for cooperation with the Soviets.<sup id="cite_ref-Ambrose_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> He even visited Warsaw in 1945. Invited by <a href="/wiki/Boles%C5%82aw_Bierut" title="Bolesław Bierut">Bolesław Bierut</a> and decorated with the <a href="/wiki/Virtuti_Militari" title="Virtuti Militari">highest military decoration</a>, he was shocked by the scale of destruction in the city.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> However, by mid-1947, as East–West tensions over economic recovery in Germany and the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Civil_War" title="Greek Civil War">Greek Civil War</a> escalated, Eisenhower agreed with a containment policy to stop Soviet expansion.<sup id="cite_ref-Ambrose_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1948_presidential_election">1948 presidential election</span></h3>
<p>In June 1943, a visiting politician had suggested to Eisenhower that he might become President of the United States after the war. Believing that a general should not participate in politics, <a href="/wiki/Merlo_J._Pusey" title="Merlo J. Pusey">Merlo J. Pusey</a> wrote that "figuratively speaking, [Eisenhower] kicked his political-minded visitor out of his office". As others asked him about his political future, Eisenhower told one that he could not imagine wanting to be considered for any political job "from dogcatcher to Grand High Supreme King of the Universe", and another that he could not serve as Army Chief of Staff if others believed he had political ambitions. In 1945 Truman told Eisenhower during the <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_Conference" title="Potsdam Conference">Potsdam Conference</a> that if desired, the president would help the general win the 1948 election,<sup id="cite_ref-pusey1956_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pusey1956-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> and in 1947 he offered to run as Eisenhower's running mate on the Democratic ticket if MacArthur won the Republican nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt20030711_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt20030711-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As the election approached, other prominent citizens and politicians from both parties urged Eisenhower to run for president. In January 1948, after learning of plans in New Hampshire to elect delegates supporting him for the forthcoming <a href="/wiki/1948_Republican_National_Convention" title="1948 Republican National Convention">Republican National Convention</a>, Eisenhower stated through the Army that he was "not available for and could not accept nomination to high political office"; "life-long professional soldiers", he wrote, "in the absence of some obvious and overriding reason, [should] abstain from seeking high political office".<sup id="cite_ref-pusey1956_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pusey1956-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Eisenhower maintained no political party affiliation during this time. Many believed he was forgoing his only opportunity to be president: Republican <a href="/wiki/Thomas_E._Dewey" title="Thomas E. Dewey">Thomas E. Dewey</a> was considered the probable winner and would presumably serve two terms, meaning that Eisenhower, at age 66 in 1956, would be too old to have another chance to run.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="President_at_Columbia_University_and_NATO_Supreme_Commander">President at Columbia University and NATO Supreme Commander</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018,_Berlin,_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018%2C_Berlin%2C_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="564" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-14059-0018,_Berlin,_Oberbefehlshaber_der_vier_Verb%C3%BCndeten.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Supreme Commanders of the <a href="/wiki/Allied_Control_Council" title="Allied Control Council">Four Powers</a> on June 5, 1945, in Berlin: <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Montgomery" title="Bernard Montgomery">Bernard Montgomery</a>, Dwight D. Eisenhower, <a href="/wiki/Georgy_Zhukov" title="Georgy Zhukov">Georgy Zhukov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean_de_Lattre_de_Tassigny" title="Jean de Lattre de Tassigny">Jean de Lattre de Tassigny</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1948 Eisenhower became President of <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a> university in New York City, where he was inducted into <a href="/wiki/Phi_Beta_Kappa" title="Phi Beta Kappa">Phi Beta Kappa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The assignment was described as not being a good fit in either direction.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> During that year Eisenhower's memoir, <i><a href="/wiki/Crusade_in_Europe" title="Crusade in Europe">Crusade in Europe</a></i>, was published.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Critics regarded it as one of the finest U.S. military memoirs, and it was a major financial success as well. Eisenhower's profit on the book was substantially aided by an unprecedented ruling<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_the_Treasury" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Department of the Treasury">U.S. Department of the Treasury</a> that Eisenhower was not a professional writer, but rather, marketing the lifetime asset of his experiences, and thus he had to pay only capital gains tax on his $635,000 advance instead of the much higher personal tax rate. This ruling saved Eisenhower about $400,000.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eisenhower's stint as the president of Columbia University was punctuated by his activity within the <a href="/wiki/Council_on_Foreign_Relations" title="Council on Foreign Relations">Council on Foreign Relations</a>, a study group he led as president concerning the political and military implications of the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_American_Assembly" title="The American Assembly">The American Assembly</a>, Eisenhower's "vision of a great cultural center where business, professional and governmental leaders could meet from time to time to discuss and reach conclusions concerning problems of a social and political nature". His biographer <a href="/wiki/Blanche_Wiesen_Cook" title="Blanche Wiesen Cook">Blanche Wiesen Cook</a> suggested that this period served as "the political education of General Eisenhower", since he had to prioritize wide-ranging educational, administrative, and financial demands for the university. Through his involvement in the Council on Foreign Relations, he also gained exposure to economic analysis, which would become the bedrock of his understanding in economic policy. "Whatever General Eisenhower knows about economics, he has learned at the study group meetings," one Aid to Europe member claimed.
</p><p>Eisenhower accepted the presidency of the university to expand his ability to promote "the American form of democracy" through education. He was clear on this point to the trustees involved in the search committee. He informed them that his main purpose was "to promote the basic concepts of education in a democracy". As a result, he was "almost incessantly" devoted to the idea of the American Assembly, a concept he developed into an institution by the end of 1950.
</p><p>Within months of beginning his tenure as the president of the university, Eisenhower was requested to advise <a href="/wiki/U.S._Secretary_of_Defense" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Secretary of Defense">U.S. Secretary of Defense</a> <a href="/wiki/James_Forrestal" title="James Forrestal">James Forrestal</a> on the unification of the armed services. About six months after his appointment, he became the informal <a href="/wiki/Chairman_of_the_Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff" title="Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff">Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff</a> in Washington. Two months later he fell ill, and he spent over a month in recovery at the <a href="/wiki/Augusta_National_Golf_Club" title="Augusta National Golf Club">Augusta National Golf Club</a>. He returned to his post in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a> in mid-May, and in July 1949 took a two-month vacation out-of-state. Because the American Assembly had begun to take shape, he traveled around the country during mid-to-late 1950, building financial support from Columbia Associates, an alumni association.
</p><p>Eisenhower was unknowingly building resentment and a reputation among the Columbia University faculty and staff as an absentee president who was using the university for his own interests. As a career military man, he naturally had little in common with the academics.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The contacts gained through university and American Assembly fund-raising activities would later become important supporters in Eisenhower's bid for the Republican party nomination and the presidency. Meanwhile, Columbia University's liberal faculty members became disenchanted with the university president's ties to oilmen and businessmen, including Leonard McCollum, the president of <a href="/wiki/Continental_Oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Continental Oil">Continental Oil</a>; Frank Abrams, the chairman of <a href="/wiki/Standard_Oil_of_New_Jersey" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard Oil of New Jersey">Standard Oil of New Jersey</a>; Bob Kleberg, the president of the King Ranch; H. J. Porter, a Texas oil executive; Bob Woodruff, the president of the <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Coca-Cola Corporation">Coca-Cola Corporation</a>; and Clarence Francis, the chairman of <a href="/wiki/General_Foods" title="General Foods">General Foods</a>.
</p><p>As the president of Columbia, Eisenhower gave voice and form to his opinions about the supremacy and difficulties of American democracy. His tenure marked his transformation from military to civilian leadership. His biographer Travis Beal Jacobs also suggested that the alienation of the Columbia faculty contributed to sharp intellectual criticism of him for many years.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The trustees of Columbia University refused to accept Eisenhower's resignation in December 1950, when he took an extended leave from the university to become the Supreme Commander of the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty_Organization" class="mw-redirect" title="North Atlantic Treaty Organization">North Atlantic Treaty Organization</a> (NATO), and he was given operational command of NATO forces in Europe. Eisenhower retired from active service as an army general on May 31, 1952, and he resumed his presidency of Columbia. He held this position until January 20, 1953, when he became the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Presidency of Dwight D. Eisenhower">President of the United States</a>.
</p><p>NATO did not have strong bipartisan support in Congress at the time that Eisenhower assumed its military command. Eisenhower advised the participating European nations that it would be incumbent upon them to demonstrate their own commitment of troops and equipment to the NATO force before such would come from the war-weary United States.
</p><p>At home, Eisenhower was more effective in making the case for NATO in Congress than the Truman administration had been. By the middle of 1951, with American and European support, NATO was a genuine military power. Nevertheless, Eisenhower thought that NATO would become a truly European alliance, with the American and Canadian commitments ending after about ten years.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidential_campaign_of_1952">Presidential campaign of 1952</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1952_United_States_presidential_election" title="1952 United States presidential election">1952 United States presidential election</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:I_Like_Ike_button,_1952.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/I_Like_Ike_button%2C_1952.png/170px-I_Like_Ike_button%2C_1952.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/I_Like_Ike_button%2C_1952.png/255px-I_Like_Ike_button%2C_1952.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/I_Like_Ike_button%2C_1952.png/340px-I_Like_Ike_button%2C_1952.png 2x" data-file-width="420" data-file-height="412" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:I_Like_Ike_button,_1952.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eisenhower button from the 1952 campaign</div></div></div>
<p>President Truman sensed a broad-based desire for an Eisenhower candidacy for president, and he again pressed him to run for the office as a Democrat in 1951. But Eisenhower voiced his disagreements with the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic Party</a> and declared himself to be a <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> A "<a href="/wiki/Draft_Eisenhower" class="mw-redirect" title="Draft Eisenhower">Draft Eisenhower</a>" movement in the Republican Party persuaded him to declare his candidacy in the 1952 presidential election to counter the candidacy of non-interventionist Senator <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Taft" title="Robert A. Taft">Robert A. Taft</a>. The effort was a long struggle; Eisenhower had to be convinced that political circumstances had created a genuine duty for him to offer himself as a candidate, and that there was a mandate from the public for him to be their President. Henry Cabot Lodge and others succeeded in convincing him, and he resigned his command at NATO in June 1952 to campaign full-time.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eisenhower defeated Taft for the nomination, having won critical delegate votes from Texas. His campaign was noted for the simple slogan "<a href="/wiki/I_Like_Ike" class="mw-redirect" title="I Like Ike">I Like Ike</a>". It was essential to his success that Eisenhower express opposition to Roosevelt's policy at Yalta and to Truman's policies in Korea and China—matters in which he had once participated.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-time_2008_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time_2008-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> In defeating Taft for the nomination, it became necessary for Eisenhower to appease the right wing Old Guard of the Republican Party; his selection of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> as the Vice-President on the ticket was designed in part for that purpose. Nixon also provided a strong anti-communist presence, as well as some youth to counter Ike's more advanced age.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1952.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/ElectoralCollege1952.svg/280px-ElectoralCollege1952.svg.png" decoding="async" width="280" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/ElectoralCollege1952.svg/420px-ElectoralCollege1952.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/ElectoralCollege1952.svg/560px-ElectoralCollege1952.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1020" data-file-height="593" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1952.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1952 electoral vote results</div></div></div>
<p>Eisenhower insisted on campaigning in the South in the general election, against the advice of his campaign team, refusing to surrender the region to the Democratic Party. The campaign strategy was dubbed "K<sub>1</sub>C<sub>2</sub>" and was intended to focus on attacking the Truman and Roosevelt administrations on three issues: Korea, Communism, and corruption. In an effort to accommodate the right, he stressed that the liberation of Eastern Europe should be by peaceful means only; he also distanced himself from his former boss President Truman.
</p><p>Two controversies tested him and his staff during the campaign, but they did not affect the campaign. One involved a report that Nixon had improperly received funds from a secret trust. Nixon <a href="/wiki/Checkers_speech" title="Checkers speech">spoke out adroitly</a> to avoid potential damage, but the matter permanently alienated the two candidates. The second issue centered on Eisenhower's relented decision to confront the controversial methods of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_McCarthy" title="Joseph McCarthy">Joseph McCarthy</a> on his home turf in a Wisconsin appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Just two weeks before the election, Eisenhower vowed to go to Korea and end the war there. He promised to maintain a strong commitment against Communism while avoiding the topic of NATO; finally, he stressed a corruption-free, frugal administration at home.
</p><p>Eisenhower defeated Democratic candidate <a href="/wiki/Adlai_Stevenson_II" title="Adlai Stevenson II">Adlai Stevenson II</a> in a landslide, with an electoral margin of 442 to 89, marking the first Republican return to the White House in 20 years.<sup id="cite_ref-time_2008_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time_2008-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> He also brought a Republican majority in the House, by eight votes, and in the Senate, evenly divided with Vice President Nixon providing Republicans the majority.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eisenhower was the last president born in the 19th century, and he was the oldest president-elect at age 62 since <a href="/wiki/James_Buchanan" title="James Buchanan">James Buchanan</a> in 1856.<sup id="cite_ref-&#39;70s_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&#39;70s-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> He was the third commanding general of the Army to serve as president, after <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">Ulysses S. Grant</a>, and the last to have not held political office prior to being president until <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> entered office in January 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1956">Election of 1956</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1956_United_States_presidential_election" title="1956 United States presidential election">1956 United States presidential election</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1956.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/ElectoralCollege1956.svg/280px-ElectoralCollege1956.svg.png" decoding="async" width="280" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/ElectoralCollege1956.svg/420px-ElectoralCollege1956.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/ElectoralCollege1956.svg/560px-ElectoralCollege1956.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1020" data-file-height="593" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1956.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1956 electoral vote results</div></div></div>
<p>The United States presidential election of 1956 was held on November 6, 1956. Eisenhower, the popular incumbent, successfully ran for re-election. The election was a re-match of 1952, as his opponent in 1956 was Stevenson, a former Illinois governor, whom Eisenhower had defeated four years earlier. Compared to the 1952 election, Eisenhower gained Kentucky, Louisiana, and West Virginia from Stevenson, while losing Missouri. His voters were less likely to bring up his leadership record. Instead what stood out this time, "was the response to personal qualities— to his sincerity, his integrity and sense of duty, his virtue as a family man, his religious devotion, and his sheer likeableness."<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Presidency_.281953.E2.80.931961.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_(1953–1961)">Presidency (1953–1961)</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Presidency of Dwight D. Eisenhower">Presidency of Dwight D. Eisenhower</a></div>
<p>Truman and Eisenhower had minimal discussions about the transition of administrations due to a complete estrangement between them as a result of campaigning.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Eisenhower selected <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Dodge" title="Joseph Dodge">Joseph M. Dodge</a> as his budget director, then asked <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Brownell_Jr." title="Herbert Brownell Jr.">Herbert Brownell Jr.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lucius_D._Clay" title="Lucius D. Clay">Lucius D. Clay</a> to make recommendations for his cabinet appointments. He accepted their recommendations without exception; they included <a href="/wiki/John_Foster_Dulles" title="John Foster Dulles">John Foster Dulles</a> and <a href="/wiki/George_M._Humphrey" title="George M. Humphrey">George M. Humphrey</a> with whom he developed his closest relationships, as well as <a href="/wiki/Oveta_Culp_Hobby" title="Oveta Culp Hobby">Oveta Culp Hobby</a>. His cabinet consisted of several corporate executives and one labor leader, and one journalist dubbed it "eight millionaires and a plumber."<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The cabinet was known for its lack of personal friends, office seekers, or experienced government administrators. He also upgraded the role of the National Security Council in planning all phases of the Cold War.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Prior to his inauguration, Eisenhower led a meeting of advisors at Pearl Harbor addressing foremost issues; agreed objectives were to balance the budget during his term, to bring the Korean War to an end, to defend vital interests at lower cost through nuclear deterrent, and to end price and wage controls.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> He also conducted the first pre-inaugural cabinet meeting in history in late 1952; he used this meeting to articulate his anti-communist Russia policy. His inaugural address was also exclusively devoted to foreign policy and included this same philosophy as well as a commitment to foreign trade and the United Nations.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dwight_D._Eisenhower,_White_House_photo_portrait,_February_1959.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_White_House_photo_portrait%2C_February_1959.jpg/220px-Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_White_House_photo_portrait%2C_February_1959.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_White_House_photo_portrait%2C_February_1959.jpg/330px-Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_White_House_photo_portrait%2C_February_1959.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_White_House_photo_portrait%2C_February_1959.jpg/440px-Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_White_House_photo_portrait%2C_February_1959.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2775" data-file-height="3354" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dwight_D._Eisenhower,_White_House_photo_portrait,_February_1959.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>February 1959 White House Portrait</div></div></div>
<p>Eisenhower made greater use of press conferences than any previous president, holding almost 200 over his two terms. He saw the benefit of maintaining a good relationship with the press, and he saw value in them as a means of direct communication with the American people.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout his presidency, Eisenhower adhered to a political philosophy of dynamic conservatism.<sup id="cite_ref-allida_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allida-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> He described himself as a "progressive conservative"<sup id="cite_ref-EisenhowerEisenhower2011_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EisenhowerEisenhower2011-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> and used terms such as "progressive moderate" and "dynamic conservatism" to describe his approach.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> He continued all the major <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a> programs still in operation, especially <a href="/wiki/Social_Security_(United_States)" title="Social Security (United States)">Social Security</a>. He expanded its programs and rolled them into the new cabinet-level agency of the Department of Health, Education and Welfare, while extending benefits to an additional ten million workers. He implemented racial integration in the Armed Services in two years, which had not been completed under Truman.<sup id="cite_ref-Miller_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miller-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When the 1954 Congressional elections approached, it became evident that the Republicans were in danger of losing their thin majority in both houses. Eisenhower was among those who blamed the Old Guard for the losses, and he took up the charge to stop suspected efforts by the right wing to take control of the GOP. He then articulated his position as a moderate, progressive Republican: "I have just one purpose… and that is to build up a strong progressive Republican Party in this country. If the right wing wants a fight, they are going to get it… before I end up, either this Republican Party will reflect progressivism or I won't be with them anymore."<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eisenhower initially planned on serving only one term, but he remained flexible in case leading Republicans wanted him to run again. He was recovering from a heart attack late in September 1955 when he met with his closest advisors to evaluate the GOP's potential candidates; the group concluded that a second term was well advised, and he announced that he would run again in February 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Eisenhower was publicly noncommittal about having Nixon as the Vice President on his ticket; the question was an especially important one in light of his heart condition. He personally favored <a href="/wiki/Robert_B._Anderson" title="Robert B. Anderson">Robert B. Anderson</a>, a Democrat who rejected his offer, so Eisenhower resolved to leave the matter in the hands of the party.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> In 1956, Eisenhower faced Adlai Stevenson again and won by an even larger landslide, with 457 of 531 electoral votes and 57.6-percent of the popular vote. The level of campaigning was curtailed out of health considerations.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eisenhower made full use of his valet, chauffeur, and secretarial support; he rarely drove or even dialed a phone number. He was an avid fisherman, golfer, painter, and bridge player, and preferred active rather than passive forms of entertainment.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> On August 26, 1959, he was aboard the maiden flight of <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_One" title="Air Force One">Air Force One</a>, which replaced the <i>Columbine</i> as the presidential aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interstate_Highway_System">Interstate Highway System</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Highway_System" title="Interstate Highway System">Interstate Highway System</a></div>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Cadillacsquareexcerpt.ogg" title="File:Cadillacsquareexcerpt.ogg">Remarks in Cadillac Square, Detroit</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="78.445714285714" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Cadillacsquareexcerpt.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/de/Cadillacsquareexcerpt.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (34 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="34078" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/de/Cadillacsquareexcerpt.ogg/Cadillacsquareexcerpt.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="92016" /><track src="https://commons.wikimedia.org/w/api.php?action=timedtext&amp;title=File%3ACadillacsquareexcerpt.ogg&amp;lang=en&amp;trackformat=srt&amp;origin=%2A" kind="subtitles" type="text/x-srt" srclang="en" label="English (en) subtitles" data-dir="ltr" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">President Eisenhower delivered remarks about the need for a new highway program at Cadillac Square in Detroit on October 29, 1954<br /><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.fhwa.dot.gov/interstate/audiotext.htm#cadillac">Text of speech excerpt</a></div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>Eisenhower was assured of an enduring achievement when he championed and signed the bill that authorized the <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Highway_System" title="Interstate Highway System">Interstate Highway System</a> in 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-economist_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-economist-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> He justified the project through the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aid_Highway_Act_of_1956" title="Federal Aid Highway Act of 1956">Federal Aid Highway Act of 1956</a> as essential to American security during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. It was believed that large cities would be targets in a possible war, so the highways were designed to facilitate their evacuation and ease military maneuvers.
</p><p>Eisenhower's goal to create improved highways was influenced by difficulties that he encountered during his involvement in the Army's 1919 <a href="/wiki/Transcontinental_Motor_Convoy" title="Transcontinental Motor Convoy">Transcontinental Motor Convoy</a>. He was assigned as an observer for the mission, which involved sending a convoy of Army vehicles coast to coast.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> His subsequent experience with the German <a href="/wiki/Reichsautobahn" title="Reichsautobahn">autobahn</a> limited-access road systems during the concluding stages of World War II convinced him of the benefits of an Interstate Highway System. The system could also be used as a runway for airplanes, which would be beneficial to war efforts. <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> put this system into place with the <a href="/wiki/Federal-Aid_Highway_Act_of_1944" title="Federal-Aid Highway Act of 1944">Federal-Aid Highway Act of 1944</a>. He thought that an interstate highway system would be beneficial for military operations and would also provide a measure of continued economic growth for the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-archivesIHS_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archivesIHS-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> The legislation initially stalled in Congress over the issuance of bonds to finance the project, but the legislative effort was renewed and Eisenhower signed the law in June 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_policy_of_the_Dwight_D._Eisenhower_administration" title="Foreign policy of the Dwight D. Eisenhower administration">Foreign policy of the Dwight D. Eisenhower administration</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Indo_US.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Indo_US.jpg/220px-Indo_US.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Indo_US.jpg/330px-Indo_US.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Indo_US.jpg/440px-Indo_US.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1953" data-file-height="1505" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Indo_US.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eisenhower with Indian Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru" title="Jawaharlal Nehru">Jawaharlal Nehru</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._President_Eisenhower_visited_TAIWAN_%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/U.S._President_Eisenhower_visited_TAIWAN_%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg/220px-U.S._President_Eisenhower_visited_TAIWAN_%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/U.S._President_Eisenhower_visited_TAIWAN_%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg/330px-U.S._President_Eisenhower_visited_TAIWAN_%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/U.S._President_Eisenhower_visited_TAIWAN_%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg/440px-U.S._President_Eisenhower_visited_TAIWAN_%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1102" data-file-height="689" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._President_Eisenhower_visited_TAIWAN_%E7%BE%8E%E5%9C%8B%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1%E8%89%BE%E6%A3%AE%E8%B1%AA%E6%96%BC1960%E5%B9%B46%E6%9C%88%E8%A8%AA%E5%95%8F%E8%87%BA%E7%81%A3%E5%8F%B0%E5%8C%97%E6%99%82%E8%88%87%E8%94%A3%E4%B8%AD%E6%AD%A3%E7%B8%BD%E7%B5%B1-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. President Eisenhower visits  the Republic of China and its <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Republic_of_China" title="President of the Republic of China">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Chiang_Kai-shek" title="Chiang Kai-shek">Chiang Kai-shek</a> in Taipei.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Eisenhower_and_Nikita_Khrushchev_1959.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/President_Eisenhower_and_Nikita_Khrushchev_1959.jpg/220px-President_Eisenhower_and_Nikita_Khrushchev_1959.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/President_Eisenhower_and_Nikita_Khrushchev_1959.jpg/330px-President_Eisenhower_and_Nikita_Khrushchev_1959.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/President_Eisenhower_and_Nikita_Khrushchev_1959.jpg/440px-President_Eisenhower_and_Nikita_Khrushchev_1959.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1298" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Eisenhower_and_Nikita_Khrushchev_1959.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soviet Premier <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a> during his 11-day U.S. visit as guest of President Eisenhower, September 1959</div></div></div>
<p>In 1953 the Republican Party's Old Guard presented Eisenhower with a dilemma by insisting he disavow the Yalta Agreements as beyond the constitutional authority of the Executive Branch; however, the death of Joseph Stalin in March 1953 made the matter a moot point.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> At this time Eisenhower gave his <a href="/wiki/Chance_for_Peace_speech" title="Chance for Peace speech">Chance for Peace speech</a> in which he attempted, unsuccessfully, to forestall the nuclear arms race with the Soviet Union by suggesting multiple opportunities presented by peaceful uses of nuclear materials. Biographer Stephen Ambrose opined that this was the best speech of Eisenhower's presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nevertheless, the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> escalated during his presidency. When the Soviet Union <a href="/wiki/RDS-37" title="RDS-37">successfully tested a hydrogen bomb</a> in late November 1955, Eisenhower, against the advice of Dulles, decided to initiate a disarmament proposal to the Soviets. In an attempt to make their refusal more difficult, he proposed that both sides agree to dedicate fissionable material away from weapons toward peaceful uses, such as power generation. This approach was labeled "<a href="/wiki/Atoms_for_Peace" title="Atoms for Peace">Atoms for Peace</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The U.N. speech was well received but the Soviets never acted upon it, due to an overarching concern for the greater stockpiles of nuclear weapons in the U.S. arsenal. Indeed, Eisenhower embarked upon a greater reliance on the use of nuclear weapons, while reducing conventional forces, and with them the overall defense budget, a policy formulated as a result of <a href="/wiki/Project_Solarium" title="Project Solarium">Project Solarium</a> and expressed in <a href="/wiki/NSC_162/2" title="NSC 162/2">NSC 162/2</a>. This approach became known as the "<a href="/wiki/New_Look_(policy)" title="New Look (policy)">New Look</a>", and was initiated with defense cuts in late 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1955 American nuclear arms policy became one aimed primarily at arms control as opposed to disarmament. The failure of negotiations over arms until 1955 was due mainly to the refusal of the Russians to permit any sort of inspections. In talks located in London that year, they expressed a willingness to discuss inspections; the tables were then turned on Eisenhower, when he responded with an unwillingness on the part of the U.S. to permit inspections. In May of that year the Russians agreed to sign a treaty giving independence to Austria, and paved the way for a Geneva summit with the U.S., U.K. and France.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> At the Geneva Conference Eisenhower presented a proposal called "Open Skies" to facilitate disarmament, which included plans for Russia and the U.S. to provide mutual access to each other's skies for open surveillance of military infrastructure. Russian leader Nikita Khrushchev dismissed the proposal out of hand.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1954 Eisenhower articulated the <a href="/wiki/Domino_theory" title="Domino theory">domino theory</a> in his outlook towards communism in Southeast Asia and also in Central America. He believed that if the communists were allowed to prevail in Vietnam, this would cause a succession of countries to fall to communism, from Laos through Malaysia and Indonesia ultimately to India. Likewise, the fall of Guatemala would end with the fall of neighboring Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> That year the loss of North Vietnam to the communists and the rejection of his proposed <a href="/wiki/European_Defence_Community" class="mw-redirect" title="European Defence Community">European Defence Community</a> (EDC) were serious defeats, but he remained optimistic in his opposition to the spread of communism, saying "Long faces don't win wars".<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> As he had threatened the French in their rejection of EDC, he afterwards moved to restore West Germany, as a full NATO partner.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> In 1954, he also induced Congress to create an Emergency Fund for International Affairs in order to support America's use of <a href="/wiki/Cultural_diplomacy" title="Cultural diplomacy">cultural diplomacy</a> to strengthen international relations throughout Europe during the cold war.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With Eisenhower's leadership and Dulles' direction, CIA activities increased under the pretense of resisting the spread of communism in poorer countries;<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> the CIA in part deposed the leaders of Iran in <a href="/wiki/1953_Iranian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1953 Iranian coup d&#39;état">Operation Ajax</a>, of Guatemala through <a href="/wiki/1954_Guatemalan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1954 Guatemalan coup d&#39;état">Operation Pbsuccess</a>, and possibly the newly independent <a href="/wiki/Congo_Crisis" title="Congo Crisis">Republic of the Congo (Léopoldville)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> In 1954 Eisenhower wanted to increase surveillance inside the Soviet Union. With Dulles' recommendation, he authorized the deployment of thirty <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">Lockheed U-2</a>'s at a cost of $35 million (equivalent to $326.54&#160;million in 2018).<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> The Eisenhower administration also planned the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion" title="Bay of Pigs Invasion">Bay of Pigs Invasion</a> to overthrow <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a> in Cuba, which <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> was left to carry out.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Space_Race">Space Race</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Dwight_D._Eisenhower,_Dr._von_Braun_and_Others.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/President_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_Dr._von_Braun_and_Others.jpg/220px-President_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_Dr._von_Braun_and_Others.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/President_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_Dr._von_Braun_and_Others.jpg/330px-President_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_Dr._von_Braun_and_Others.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/President_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_Dr._von_Braun_and_Others.jpg/440px-President_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_Dr._von_Braun_and_Others.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1277" data-file-height="1083" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Dwight_D._Eisenhower,_Dr._von_Braun_and_Others.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>President Eisenhower with <a href="/wiki/Wernher_von_Braun" title="Wernher von Braun">Wernher von Braun</a>, 1960</div></div></div>
<p>Eisenhower and the CIA had known since at least January 1957, nine months before Sputnik, that Russia had the capability to launch a small payload into orbit and was likely to do so within a year.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> He may also privately have welcomed the Russian satellite for its legal implications: By launching a satellite, Russia had in effect acknowledged that space was open to anyone who could access it, without needing permission from other nations.
</p><p>On the whole, Eisenhower's support of the nation's fledgling space program was officially modest until the Soviet launch of <a href="/wiki/Sputnik" class="mw-redirect" title="Sputnik">Sputnik</a> in 1957, gaining the Cold War enemy enormous prestige around the world. He then launched a national campaign that funded not just space exploration but a major strengthening of science and higher education. The Eisenhower administration determined to adopt a non-aggressive policy that would allow "space-crafts of any state to overfly all states, a region free of military posturing and launch Earth satellites to explore space".<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> His <a href="/wiki/Treaty_on_Open_Skies" title="Treaty on Open Skies">Open Skies</a> Policy attempted to legitimize illegal <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">Lockheed U-2</a> flyovers and <a href="/wiki/Project_Genetrix" title="Project Genetrix">Project Genetrix</a> while paving the way for spy satellite technology to orbit over sovereign territory,<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> however <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Bulganin" title="Nikolai Bulganin">Nikolai Bulganin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a> declined Eisenhower's proposal at the Geneva conference in July 1955.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> In response to Sputnik being launched in October 1957, Eisenhower created <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> as a civilian space agency in October 1958, signed a landmark science education law, and improved relations with American scientists.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Fear spread through the United States that the Soviet Union would invade and spread <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communism</a>, so Eisenhower wanted to not only create a surveillance satellite to detect any threats but ballistic missiles that would protect the United States. In strategic terms, it was Eisenhower who devised the American basic strategy of nuclear deterrence based upon the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_triad" title="Nuclear triad">triad</a> of B-52 bombers, land-based intercontinental ballistic missiles (ICBMs), and Polaris submarine-launched ballistic missiles (SLBMs).<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>NASA planners projected that human spaceflight would pull the United States ahead in the Space Race as well as accomplishing their long time goal; however, in 1960, an Ad Hoc Panel on Man-in-Space concluded that "man-in-space can not be justified" and was too costly.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Eisenhower later resented the space program and its gargantuan price tag—he was quoted as saying, "Anyone who would spend $40 billion in a race to the moon for national prestige is nuts."<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Korean_War.2C_Free_China_and_Red_China"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Korean_War,_Free_China_and_Red_China">Korean War, Free China and Red China</span></h4>
<p>In late 1952 Eisenhower went to Korea and discovered a military and political stalemate. Once in office, when the Chinese communists began a buildup in the Kaesong sanctuary, he threatened to use nuclear force if an armistice was not concluded. His earlier military reputation in Europe was effective with the Chinese communists.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> The National Security Council, the Joint Chiefs of Staff, and the Strategic Air Command (SAC) devised detailed plans for nuclear war against Red China.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> With the death of Stalin in early March 1953, Russian support for a Chinese communists hard-line weakened and Red China decided to compromise on the prisoner issue.<sup id="cite_ref-Ambrose_1984,_p._106–7_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose_1984,_p._106–7-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1951_Chung_Baik_Eisenhower.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/1951_Chung_Baik_Eisenhower.jpg/220px-1951_Chung_Baik_Eisenhower.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f7/1951_Chung_Baik_Eisenhower.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1951_Chung_Baik_Eisenhower.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eisenhower in Korea with General <a href="/wiki/Chung_Il-kwon" title="Chung Il-kwon">Chung Il-kwon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Baik_Seon-yup" class="mw-redirect" title="Baik Seon-yup">Baik Seon-yup</a>, 1952</div></div></div>
<p>In July 1953, an armistice took effect with Korea divided along <a href="/wiki/Military_Demarcation_Line" title="Military Demarcation Line">approximately the same boundary as in 1950</a>. The armistice and boundary remain in effect today. The armistice, concluded despite opposition from Secretary Dulles, South Korean President <a href="/wiki/Syngman_Rhee" title="Syngman Rhee">Syngman Rhee</a>, and also within Eisenhower's party, has been described by biographer Ambrose as the greatest achievement of the administration. Eisenhower had the insight to realize that unlimited war in the nuclear age was unthinkable, and limited war unwinnable.<sup id="cite_ref-Ambrose_1984,_p._106–7_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose_1984,_p._106–7-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A point of emphasis in Ike's campaign had been his endorsement of a policy of liberation from communism as opposed to a policy of containment. This remained his preference despite the armistice with Korea.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> Throughout his terms Eisenhower took a hard-line attitude toward Red China, as demanded by conservative Republicans, with the goal of driving a wedge between Red China and the Soviet Union.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eisenhower continued Truman's policy of recognizing the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a> (Free China) as the legitimate government of China, not the Beijing regime. There were localized flare-ups when the People's Liberation Army began shelling the islands of <a href="/wiki/Quemoy" class="mw-redirect" title="Quemoy">Quemoy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Matsu_Islands" title="Matsu Islands">Matsu</a> in September 1954. Eisenhower received recommendations embracing every variation of response to the aggression of the Chinese communists. He thought it essential to have every possible option available to him as the crisis unfolded.<sup id="cite_ref-Ambrose_1984,_p._231_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose_1984,_p._231-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Sino-American Mutual Defense Treaty with the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a> was signed in December 1954. He requested and secured from Congress their "Free China Resolution" in January 1955, which gave Eisenhower unprecedented power in advance to use military force at any level of his choosing in defense of Free China and the Pescadores. The Resolution bolstered the morale of the Chinese nationalists, and signaled to Beijing that the U.S. was committed to holding the line.<sup id="cite_ref-Ambrose_1984,_p._231_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose_1984,_p._231-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eisenhower openly threatened the Chinese communists with use of nuclear weapons, authorizing a series of bomb tests labeled <a href="/wiki/Operation_Teapot" title="Operation Teapot">Operation Teapot</a>. Nevertheless, he left the Chinese communists guessing as to the exact nature of his nuclear response. This allowed Eisenhower to accomplish all of his objectives—the end of this communist encroachment, the retention of the Islands by the Chinese nationalists and continued peace.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Defense of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of China">Republic of China</a> from an invasion remains a core American policy.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the end of 1954 Eisenhower's military and foreign policy experts—the NSC, JCS and State Dept.—had unanimously urged him, on no less than five occasions, to launch an atomic attack against Red China; yet he consistently refused to do so and felt a distinct sense of accomplishment in having sufficiently confronted communism while keeping world peace.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Middle_East_and_Eisenhower_doctrine">The Middle East and Eisenhower doctrine</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ShahEisenhower.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/ShahEisenhower.jpg/200px-ShahEisenhower.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="246" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/ShahEisenhower.jpg/300px-ShahEisenhower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/35/ShahEisenhower.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="491" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ShahEisenhower.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eisenhower with the Shah of Iran, <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Reza_Pahlavi" title="Mohammad Reza Pahlavi">Mohammad Reza Pahlavi</a> (1959)</div></div></div>
<p>Even before he was inaugurated Eisenhower accepted a request from the British government to restore the Shah of Iran (<a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Reza_Pahlavi" title="Mohammad Reza Pahlavi">Mohammad Reza Pahlavi</a>) to power. He therefore <a href="/wiki/1953_Iranian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1953 Iranian coup d&#39;état">authorized the Central Intelligence Agency</a> to overthrow Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Mosaddegh" title="Mohammad Mosaddegh">Mohammad Mosaddegh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> This resulted in an increased strategic control over Iranian oil by U.S. and British companies.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 1956, Eisenhower forced an end to the combined British, French and Israeli invasion of Egypt in response to the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a>, receiving praise from Egyptian president <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a>. Simultaneously he condemned the brutal Soviet invasion of <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_People%27s_Republic" title="Hungarian People&#39;s Republic">Hungary</a> in response to the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956" title="Hungarian Revolution of 1956">Hungarian Revolution of 1956</a>. He publicly disavowed his allies at the United Nations, and used financial and diplomatic pressure to make them withdraw from Egypt.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> Eisenhower explicitly defended his strong position against Britain and France in his memoirs, which were published in 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the Suez Crisis the United States became the protector of unstable friendly governments in the Middle East via the "<a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_Doctrine" title="Eisenhower Doctrine">Eisenhower Doctrine</a>". Designed by Secretary of State Dulles, it held the U.S. would be "prepared to use armed force&#160;... [to counter] aggression from any country controlled by international communism". Further, the United States would provide economic and military aid and, if necessary, use military force to stop the spread of communism in the Middle East.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenhower_and_Nixon_at_Dinner_with_King_Saud.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Eisenhower_and_Nixon_at_Dinner_with_King_Saud.jpg/220px-Eisenhower_and_Nixon_at_Dinner_with_King_Saud.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Eisenhower_and_Nixon_at_Dinner_with_King_Saud.jpg/330px-Eisenhower_and_Nixon_at_Dinner_with_King_Saud.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Eisenhower_and_Nixon_at_Dinner_with_King_Saud.jpg/440px-Eisenhower_and_Nixon_at_Dinner_with_King_Saud.jpg 2x" data-file-width="635" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenhower_and_Nixon_at_Dinner_with_King_Saud.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eisenhower and Vice President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> with their host, King <a href="/wiki/Saud_of_Saudi_Arabia" title="Saud of Saudi Arabia">Saud of Saudi Arabia</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Mayflower_Hotel" title="Mayflower Hotel">Mayflower Hotel</a> (1957)</div></div></div>
<p>Eisenhower applied the doctrine in 1957–58 by dispensing economic aid to shore up the Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, and by encouraging <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>'s neighbors to consider military operations against it. More dramatically, in July 1958, he sent 15,000 Marines and soldiers to <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a> as part of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Blue_Bat" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Blue Bat">Operation Blue Bat</a>, a non-combat peace-keeping mission to stabilize the pro-Western government and to prevent a radical revolution from sweeping over that country.<sup id="cite_ref-Little_1996_27–54_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Little_1996_27–54-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The mission proved a success and the Marines departed three months later. The deployment came in response to the urgent request of Lebanese president <a href="/wiki/Camille_Chamoun" title="Camille Chamoun">Camille Chamoun</a> after sectarian violence had erupted in the country. Washington considered the military intervention successful since it brought about regional stability, weakened Soviet influence, and intimidated the Egyptian and Syrian governments, whose anti-West political position had hardened after the Suez Crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-Little_1996_27–54_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Little_1996_27–54-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most Arab countries were skeptical about the "Eisenhower doctrine" because they considered "Zionist imperialism" the real danger. However, they did take the opportunity to obtain free money and weapons. Egypt and Syria, supported by the Soviet Union, openly opposed the initiative. However, Egypt received American aid until the <a href="/wiki/Six-Day_War" title="Six-Day War">Six-Day War</a> in 1967.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> deepened, Dulles sought to isolate the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> by building regional alliances of nations against it. Critics sometimes called it "<a href="/wiki/Pactomania" title="Pactomania">pacto-mania</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</span></h4>
<p>Early in 1953, the French asked Eisenhower for help in <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">French Indochina</a> against the Communists, supplied from China, who were fighting the <a href="/wiki/First_Indochina_War" title="First Indochina War">First Indochina War</a>. Eisenhower sent Lt. General <a href="/wiki/John_W._O%27Daniel" title="John W. O&#39;Daniel">John W. "Iron Mike" O'Daniel</a> to Vietnam to study and assess the French forces there.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Chief of Staff <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Ridgway" title="Matthew Ridgway">Matthew Ridgway</a> dissuaded the President from intervening by presenting a comprehensive estimate of the massive military deployment that would be necessary. Eisenhower stated prophetically that "this war would absorb our troops by divisions."<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eisenhower did provide France with bombers and non-combat personnel. After a few months with no success by the French, he added other aircraft to drop <a href="/wiki/Napalm" title="Napalm">napalm</a> for clearing purposes. Further requests for assistance from the French were agreed to but only on conditions Eisenhower knew were impossible to meet&#160;– allied participation and congressional approval.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> When the French fortress of <a href="/wiki/Dien_Bien_Phu" class="mw-redirect" title="Dien Bien Phu">Dien Bien Phu</a> fell to the Vietnamese Communists in May 1954, Eisenhower refused to intervene despite urgings from the Chairman of the Joint Chiefs, the Vice President and the head of NCS.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eisenhower responded to the French defeat with the formation of the <a href="/wiki/SEATO" class="mw-redirect" title="SEATO">SEATO</a> (Southeast Asia Treaty Organization) Alliance with the U.K., France, New Zealand and Australia in defense of Vietnam against communism. At that time the French and Chinese reconvened Geneva peace talks; Eisenhower agreed the U.S. would participate only as an observer. After France and the Communists agreed to a partition of Vietnam, Eisenhower rejected the agreement, offering military and economic aid to southern Vietnam.<sup id="cite_ref-Nofi_Albert_p_257_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nofi_Albert_p_257-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> Ambrose argues that Eisenhower, by not participating in the Geneva agreement, had kept the U.S. out of Vietnam; nevertheless, with the formation of SEATO, he had in the end put the U.S. back into the conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In late 1954, <a href="/wiki/J._Lawton_Collins" title="J. Lawton Collins">Gen. J. Lawton Collins</a> was made ambassador to "Free Vietnam" (the term <a href="/wiki/South_Vietnam" title="South Vietnam">South Vietnam</a> came into use in 1955), effectively elevating the country to sovereign status. Collins' instructions were to support the leader <a href="/wiki/Ngo_Dinh_Diem" title="Ngo Dinh Diem">Ngo Dinh Diem</a> in subverting communism, by helping him to build an army and wage a military campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> In February 1955, Eisenhower dispatched the first American soldiers to Vietnam as military advisors to Diem's army. After Diem announced the formation of the Republic of Vietnam (RVN, commonly known as South Vietnam) in October, Eisenhower immediately recognized the new state and offered military, economic, and technical assistance.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the years that followed, Eisenhower increased the number of U.S. military advisors in South Vietnam to 900 men.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> This was due to <a href="/wiki/North_Vietnam" title="North Vietnam">North Vietnam</a>'s support of "uprisings" in the south and concern the nation would fall.<sup id="cite_ref-Nofi_Albert_p_257_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nofi_Albert_p_257-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> In May 1957 Diem, then <a href="/wiki/Leaders_of_South_Vietnam" title="Leaders of South Vietnam">President of South Vietnam</a>, made a <a href="/wiki/State_visit" title="State visit">state visit</a> to the United States for ten days. President Eisenhower pledged his continued support, and a parade was held in Diem's honor in New York City. Although Diem was publicly praised, in private Secretary of State John Foster Dulles conceded that Diem had been selected because there were no better alternatives.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the election of November 1960, Eisenhower in briefing with <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> pointed out the communist threat in Southeast Asia as requiring prioritization in the next administration. Eisenhower told Kennedy he considered <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a> "the cork in the bottle" with regard to the regional threat.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1960_U-2_incident">1960 U-2 incident</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1960_U-2_incident" title="1960 U-2 incident">1960 U-2 incident</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Air_Force_U-2_(2139646280).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/US_Air_Force_U-2_%282139646280%29.jpg/250px-US_Air_Force_U-2_%282139646280%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/US_Air_Force_U-2_%282139646280%29.jpg/375px-US_Air_Force_U-2_%282139646280%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/US_Air_Force_U-2_%282139646280%29.jpg/500px-US_Air_Force_U-2_%282139646280%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Air_Force_U-2_(2139646280).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">U-2</a> reconnaissance aircraft in flight</div></div></div>
<p>On May 1, 1960, a U.S. one-man <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">U-2</a> spy plane was reportedly shot down at high altitude over <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> airspace. The flight was made to gain photo intelligence before the scheduled opening of an East-West summit conference, which had been scheduled in Paris, 15 days later.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Captain <a href="/wiki/Francis_Gary_Powers" title="Francis Gary Powers">Francis Gary Powers</a> had bailed out of his aircraft and was captured after parachuting down onto Russian soil.
