Four days after Powers disappeared, the Eisenhower Administration had NASA issue a very detailed press release noting that an aircraft had "gone missing" north of Turkey. It speculated that the pilot might have fallen unconscious while the autopilot was still engaged, and falsely claimed that "the pilot reported over the emergency frequency that he was experiencing oxygen difficulties."<sup id="cite_ref-Russian_officer_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Russian_officer-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Soviet Premier <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a> announced that a "spy-plane" had been shot down but intentionally made no reference to the pilot. As a result, the Eisenhower Administration, thinking the pilot had died in the crash, authorized the release of a cover story claiming that the plane was a "weather research aircraft" which had unintentionally strayed into Soviet airspace after the pilot had radioed "difficulties with his oxygen equipment" while flying over Turkey.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> The Soviets put Captain Powers on trial and displayed parts of the U-2, which had been recovered almost fully intact.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Cold_War_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Cold_War-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Four Power Paris Summit in May 1960 with Eisenhower, Nikita Khrushchev, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a> collapsed because of the incident. Eisenhower refused to accede to Khrushchev's demands that he apologize. Therefore, Khrushchev would not take part in the summit. Up until this event, Eisenhower felt he had been making progress towards better relations with the Soviet Union. Nuclear arms reduction and Berlin were to have been discussed at the summit. Eisenhower stated it had all been ruined because of that "stupid U-2 business".<sup id="cite_ref-The_Cold_War_205-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Cold_War-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The affair was an embarrassment for United States prestige. Further, the Senate Foreign Relations Committee held a lengthy inquiry into the U-2 incident.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Cold_War_205-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Cold_War-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> In Russia, Captain Powers made a forced confession and apology. On August 19, 1960, Powers was convicted of espionage and sentenced to imprisonment. On February 10, 1962, Powers was exchanged for <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Abel" title="Rudolf Abel">Rudolf Abel</a> in Berlin and returned to the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-Russian_officer_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Russian_officer-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights">Civil rights</span></h3>
<p>While President Truman had begun the process of <a href="/wiki/Desegregation" class="mw-redirect" title="Desegregation">desegregating</a> the <a href="/wiki/Armed_Forces_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Armed Forces of the United States">Armed Forces</a> in 1948, actual implementation had been slow. Eisenhower made clear his stance in his first <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union_address" class="mw-redirect" title="State of the Union address">State of the Union address</a> in February 1953, saying "I propose to use whatever authority exists in the office of the President to end segregation in the District of Columbia, including the Federal Government, and any segregation in the Armed Forces".<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> When he encountered opposition from the services, he used government control of military spending to force the change through, stating "Wherever Federal Funds are expended&#160;..., I do not see how any American can justify&#160;... a discrimination in the expenditure of those funds".<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When <a href="/wiki/Robert_B._Anderson" title="Robert B. Anderson">Robert B. Anderson</a>, Eisenhower's first <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_the_Navy" class="mw-redirect" title="Secretary of the Navy">Secretary of the Navy</a>, argued that the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Navy" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Navy">U.S. Navy</a> must recognize the "customs and usages prevailing in certain geographic areas of our country which the Navy had no part in creating," Eisenhower overruled him: "We have not taken and we shall not take a single backward step. There must be no second class citizens in this country."<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The administration declared <a href="/wiki/Racial_discrimination" title="Racial discrimination">racial discrimination</a> a <a href="/wiki/National_security" title="National security">national security</a> issue, as Communists around the world used the racial discrimination and history of violence in the U.S. as a point of propaganda attack.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eisenhower told <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia" class="mw-redirect" title="District of Columbia">District of Columbia</a> officials to make Washington a model for the rest of the country in integrating black and white public school children.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> He proposed to Congress the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1957" title="Civil Rights Act of 1957">Civil Rights Act of 1957</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1960" title="Civil Rights Act of 1960">of 1960</a> and signed those acts into law. The 1957 act for the first time established a permanent civil rights office inside the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Justice" title="United States Department of Justice">Justice Department</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil Rights Commission">Civil Rights Commission</a> to hear testimony about abuses of voting rights. Although both acts were much weaker than subsequent civil rights legislation, they constituted the first significant civil rights acts <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1875" title="Civil Rights Act of 1875">since 1875</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1957 the state of <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a> refused to honor a federal court order to integrate their public school system stemming from the <i><a href="/wiki/Brown_v._Board_of_Education" title="Brown v. Board of Education">Brown</a></i> decision. Eisenhower demanded that Arkansas governor <a href="/wiki/Orval_Faubus" title="Orval Faubus">Orval Faubus</a> obey the court order. When Faubus balked, the president placed the <a href="/wiki/Arkansas_Army_National_Guard" title="Arkansas Army National Guard">Arkansas National Guard</a> under federal control and sent in the <a href="/wiki/101st_Airborne_Division" title="101st Airborne Division">101st Airborne Division</a>. They escorted and protected <a href="/wiki/Little_Rock_Nine" title="Little Rock Nine">nine black students</a>' entry to <a href="/wiki/Little_Rock_Central_High_School" title="Little Rock Central High School">Little Rock Central High School</a>, an all-white public school, for the first time since the <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" class="mw-redirect" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction Era</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Martin Luther King Jr.">Martin Luther King Jr.</a> wrote to Eisenhower to thank him for his actions, writing "The overwhelming majority of southerners, Negro and white, stand firmly behind your resolute action to restore law and order in Little Rock".<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eisenhower's administration contributed to the McCarthyist <a href="/wiki/Lavender_Scare" class="mw-redirect" title="Lavender Scare">Lavender Scare</a><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> with President Eisenhower issuing his <a href="/wiki/Executive_Order_10450" title="Executive Order 10450">Executive Order 10450</a> in 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> During Eisenhower's presidency thousands of lesbian and gay applicants were barred from federal employment and over 5,000 federal employees were fired under suspicions of being homosexual.<sup id="cite_ref-documenting_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-documenting-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> From 1947 to 1961 the number of firings based on sexual orientation were far greater than those for membership in the Communist party,<sup id="cite_ref-documenting_217-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-documenting-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> and government officials intentionally campaigned to make "homosexual" synonymous with "Communist traitor" such that LGBT people were treated as a national security threat stemming from the belief they were susceptible to blackmail and exploitation.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_Congress">Relations with Congress</span></h3>
<p>Eisenhower had a Republican Congress for only his first two years in office; in the Senate, the Republican majority was by a one-vote margin. Senator <a href="/wiki/Robert_A._Taft" title="Robert A. Taft">Robert A. Taft</a> assisted the President greatly in working with the Old Guard, and was sorely missed when his death (in July 1953) left Eisenhower with his successor <a href="/wiki/William_Knowland" title="William Knowland">William Knowland</a>, whom Eisenhower disliked.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This prevented Eisenhower from openly condemning Joseph McCarthy's highly criticized methods against communism. To facilitate relations with Congress, Eisenhower decided to ignore McCarthy's controversies and thereby deprive them of more energy from involvement of the White House. This position drew criticism from a number of corners.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> In late 1953, McCarthy declared on national television that the employment of communists within the government was a menace and would be a pivotal issue in the <a href="/wiki/1954_United_States_Senate_elections" title="1954 United States Senate elections">1954 Senate elections</a>. Eisenhower was urged to respond directly and specify the various measures he had taken to purge the government of communists.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Among Eisenhower's objectives in not directly confronting McCarthy was to prevent McCarthy from dragging the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Atomic_Energy_Commission" title="United States Atomic Energy Commission">Atomic Energy Commission</a> (AEC) into McCarthy's witch hunt for communists, which would interfere with, and perhaps delay, the AEC's important work on <a href="/wiki/H-bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="H-bomb">H-bombs</a>. The administration had discovered through its own investigations that one of the leading scientists on the AEC, <a href="/wiki/J._Robert_Oppenheimer" title="J. Robert Oppenheimer">J. Robert Oppenheimer</a>, had urged that the H-bomb work be delayed. Eisenhower removed him from the agency and revoked his security clearance, though he knew this would create fertile ground for McCarthy.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 1955, McCarthy threatened to issue subpoenas to White House personnel. Eisenhower was furious, and issued an order as follows: "It is essential to efficient and effective administration that employees of the Executive Branch be in a position to be completely candid in advising with each other on official matters&#160;... it is not in the public interest that any of their conversations or communications, or any documents or reproductions, concerning such advice be disclosed." This was an unprecedented step by Eisenhower to protect communication beyond the confines of a cabinet meeting, and soon became a tradition known as <a href="/wiki/Executive_privilege" title="Executive privilege">executive privilege</a>. Ike's denial of McCarthy's access to his staff reduced McCarthy's hearings to rants about trivial matters, and contributed to his ultimate downfall.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In early 1954, the Old Guard put forward a constitutional amendment, called the <a href="/wiki/Bricker_Amendment" title="Bricker Amendment">Bricker Amendment</a>, which would curtail international agreements by the Chief Executive, such as the <a href="/wiki/Yalta_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Yalta Agreement">Yalta Agreements</a>. Eisenhower opposed the measure.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> The Old Guard agreed with Eisenhower on the development and ownership of nuclear reactors by private enterprises, which the Democrats opposed. The President succeeded in getting legislation creating a system of licensure for nuclear plants by the AEC.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Democrats gained a majority in both houses in the 1954 election.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Eisenhower had to work with the Democratic Majority Leader <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">Lyndon B. Johnson</a> (later U.S. president) in the Senate and Speaker <a href="/wiki/Sam_Rayburn" title="Sam Rayburn">Sam Rayburn</a> in the House, both from Texas. <a href="/wiki/Joseph_W._Martin_Jr." title="Joseph W. Martin Jr.">Joe Martin</a>, the Republican Speaker from 1947 to 1949 and again from 1953 to 1955, wrote that Eisenhower "never surrounded himself with assistants who could solve political problems with professional skill. There were exceptions, <a href="/wiki/Leonard_W._Hall" title="Leonard W. Hall">Leonard W. Hall</a>, for example, who as chairman of the <a href="/wiki/Republican_National_Committee" title="Republican National Committee">Republican National Committee</a> tried to open the administration's eyes to the political facts of life, with occasional success. However, these exceptions were not enough to right the balance."<sup id="cite_ref-martin_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-martin-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Speaker Martin concluded that Eisenhower worked too much through subordinates in dealing with Congress, with results, "often the reverse of what he has desired" because Members of Congress, "resent having some young fellow who was picked up by the White House without ever having been elected to office himself coming around and telling them 'The Chief wants this'. The administration never made use of many Republicans of consequence whose services in one form or another would have been available for the asking."<sup id="cite_ref-martin_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-martin-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Supreme_Court">Supreme Court</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower_Supreme_Court_candidates" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower Supreme Court candidates">Dwight D. Eisenhower Supreme Court candidates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower_judicial_appointments" class="mw-redirect" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower judicial appointments">Dwight D. Eisenhower judicial appointments</a></div>
<p>Eisenhower appointed the following <a href="/wiki/List_of_Justices_of_the_Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="List of Justices of the Supreme Court of the United States">Justices</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a>:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Earl_Warren" title="Earl Warren">Earl Warren</a>, 1953 (Chief Justice)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/John_Marshall_Harlan_II" class="mw-redirect" title="John Marshall Harlan II">John Marshall Harlan II</a>, 1954</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/William_J._Brennan" class="mw-redirect" title="William J. Brennan">William J. Brennan</a>, 1956</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Charles_Evans_Whittaker" title="Charles Evans Whittaker">Charles Evans Whittaker</a>, 1957</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Potter_Stewart" title="Potter Stewart">Potter Stewart</a>, 1958</li></ul>
<p>Whittaker was unsuited for the role and soon retired. Stewart and Harlan were conservative Republicans, while Brennan was a Democrat who became a leading voice for liberalism.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> In selecting a Chief Justice, Eisenhower looked for an experienced jurist who could appeal to liberals in the party as well as law-and-order conservatives, noting privately that Warren "represents the kind of political, economic, and social thinking that I believe we need on the Supreme Court&#160;... He has a national name for integrity, uprightness, and courage that, again, I believe we need on the Court".<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> In the next few years Warren led the Court in a series of liberal decisions that revolutionized the role of the Court.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="States_admitted_to_the_Union">States admitted to the Union</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>&#160;– January 3, 1959 49th state</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>&#160;– August 21, 1959 50th state</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_issues">Health issues</span></h3>
<p>Eisenhower began chain smoking cigarettes at West Point, often three or four packs a day. He joked that he "gave [himself] an order" to stop <a href="/wiki/Cold_turkey" title="Cold turkey">cold turkey</a> in 1949. But Evan Thomas says the true story was more complex. At first he removed cigarettes and ashtrays, but that did not work. He told a friend:
</p>
<dl><dd>I decided to make a game of the whole business and try to achieve a feeling of some superiority&#160;... So I stuffed cigarettes in every pocket, put them around my office on the desk&#160;... [and] made it a practice to offer a cigarette to anyone who came in&#160;... while mentally reminding myself as I sat down, "I do not have to do what that poor fellow is doing."<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>He was the first president to release information about his health and medical records while in office, but people around him deliberately misled the public about his health. On September 24, 1955, while vacationing in Colorado, he had a serious heart attack.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Howard_McCrum_Snyder" title="Howard McCrum Snyder">Dr. Howard Snyder</a>, his personal physician, misdiagnosed the symptoms as indigestion, and failed to call in the help that was urgently needed. Snyder later falsified his own records to cover his blunder and to protect Eisenhower's need to portray he was healthy enough to do his job.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The heart attack required six weeks' hospitalization, during which time Nixon, Dulles, and <a href="/wiki/Sherman_Adams" title="Sherman Adams">Sherman Adams</a> assumed administrative duties and provided communication with the President.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> He was treated by Dr. <a href="/wiki/Paul_Dudley_White" title="Paul Dudley White">Paul Dudley White</a>, a cardiologist with a national reputation, who regularly informed the press of the President's progress. Instead of eliminating him as a candidate for a second term as President, his physician recommended a second term as essential to his recovery.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As a consequence of his heart attack, Eisenhower developed a left ventricular <a href="/wiki/Aneurysm" title="Aneurysm">aneurysm</a>, which was in turn the cause of a mild stroke on November 25, 1957. This incident occurred during a cabinet meeting when Eisenhower suddenly found himself unable to speak or move his right hand. The stroke had caused <a href="/wiki/Aphasia" title="Aphasia">aphasia</a>. The president also suffered from <a href="/wiki/Crohn%27s_disease" title="Crohn&#39;s disease">Crohn's disease</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> chronic inflammatory condition of the intestine,<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> which necessitated surgery for a bowel obstruction on June 9, 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> To treat the intestinal block, surgeons bypassed about ten inches of his <a href="/wiki/Small_intestine" title="Small intestine">small intestine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> His scheduled meeting with <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_India" title="Prime Minister of India">Indian Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru" title="Jawaharlal Nehru">Jawaharlal Nehru</a> was postponed so he could recover at his farm.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> He was still recovering from this operation during the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a>. Eisenhower's health issues forced him to give up smoking and make some changes to his dietary habits, but he still indulged in alcohol. During a visit to England he complained of dizziness and had to have his blood pressure checked on August 29, 1959; however, before dinner at Chequers on the next day his doctor General Howard Snyder recalled Eisenhower "drank several gin-and-tonics, and one or two gins on the rocks&#160;... three or four wines with the dinner".<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The last three years of Eisenhower's second term in office were ones of relatively good health. Eventually after leaving the White House, he suffered several additional and ultimately crippling heart attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-drzebra_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-drzebra-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> A severe heart attack in August 1965 largely ended his participation in public affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> In August 1966 he began to show symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Cholecystitis" title="Cholecystitis">cholecystitis</a>, for which he underwent surgery on December 12, 1966, when his gallbladder was removed, containing 16 gallstones.<sup id="cite_ref-drzebra_244-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-drzebra-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> After Eisenhower's death in 1969 (see below), an autopsy unexpectedly revealed an adrenal <a href="/wiki/Pheochromocytoma" title="Pheochromocytoma">pheochromocytoma</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> a benign adrenaline-secreting tumor that may have made the President more vulnerable to heart disease. Eisenhower suffered seven heart attacks from 1955 until his death.<sup id="cite_ref-drzebra_244-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-drzebra-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_presidency">End of presidency</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dwight_D._Eisenhower,_official_Presidential_portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_Presidential_portrait.jpg/220px-Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_Presidential_portrait.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="281" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_Presidential_portrait.jpg/330px-Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_Presidential_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_Presidential_portrait.jpg/440px-Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_official_Presidential_portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2345" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dwight_D._Eisenhower,_official_Presidential_portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The official <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> portrait of Dwight D. Eisenhower by <a href="/wiki/James_Anthony_Wills" title="James Anthony Wills">James Anthony Wills</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Twenty-second_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-second Amendment to the United States Constitution">22nd Amendment to the U.S. Constitution</a> was ratified in 1951, and it set term limits to the presidency of two terms. Truman as the incumbent was not covered. Eisenhower became the first U.S. president constitutionally prevented from running for re-election to a third term.
</p><p>Eisenhower was also the first outgoing President to come under the protection of the <a href="/wiki/Former_Presidents_Act" title="Former Presidents Act">Former Presidents Act</a>; two living former Presidents, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a>, left office before the Act was passed. Under the act, Eisenhower was entitled to receive a lifetime pension, state-provided staff and a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secret_Service" title="United States Secret Service">Secret Service</a> detail.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1960 election to choose his successor, Eisenhower endorsed Nixon over Democrat <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>. He told friends, "I will do almost anything to avoid turning my chair and country over to Kennedy."<sup id="cite_ref-time_2008_116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time_2008-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> He actively campaigned for Nixon in the final days, although he may have done Nixon some harm. When asked by reporters at the end of a televised press conference to list one of Nixon's policy ideas he had adopted, Eisenhower joked, "If you give me a week, I might think of one. I don't remember." Kennedy's campaign used the quote in one of its campaign commercials. Nixon narrowly lost to Kennedy. Eisenhower, who was the oldest president in history at that time (then 70), was succeeded by the youngest elected president, as Kennedy was 43.<sup id="cite_ref-time_2008_116-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time_2008-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was originally intended for President Eisenhower to have a more active role in the campaign as he wanted to respond to attacks Kennedy made on his administration. However, First Lady Mamie Eisenhower expressed concern to Second Lady Pat Nixon about the strain campaigning would put on his heart and wanted the President to back out of it without letting him know of her intervention. Vice President Nixon himself also received concern from White House physician Major General Howard Snyder, who informed him that he could not approve a heavy campaign schedule for the President and his health had been exacerbated by Kennedy's attacks. Nixon then convinced Eisenhower not to go ahead with the expanded campaign schedule and limit himself to the original schedule. Nixon reflected that if Eisenhower had carried out his expanded campaign schedule he might have had a decisive impact on the outcome of the election, especially in states that Kennedy won with razor-thin margins. It was years later before Mamie told Dwight why Nixon changed his mind on Dwight's campaigning.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_2" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="930.26696145125" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Eisenhower_farewell_address.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/90/Eisenhower_farewell_address.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (112 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="111848" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/90/Eisenhower_farewell_address.ogg/Eisenhower_farewell_address.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="182432" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenhower_farewell_address.ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eisenhower's farewell address, January 17, 1961. Length 15:30.</div></div></div>
<p>On January 17, 1961, Eisenhower gave his final televised Address to the Nation from the <a href="/wiki/Oval_Office" title="Oval Office">Oval Office</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DDEFarewell_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DDEFarewell-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> In his <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower%27s_farewell_address" title="Eisenhower&#39;s farewell address">farewell speech</a>, Eisenhower raised the issue of the Cold War and role of the U.S. armed forces. He described the Cold War: "We face a hostile ideology global in scope, atheistic in character, ruthless in purpose and insidious in method&#160;..." and warned about what he saw as unjustified government spending proposals and continued with a warning that "we must guard against the acquisition of unwarranted influence, whether sought or unsought, by the <a href="/wiki/Military%E2%80%93industrial_complex" title="Military–industrial complex">military–industrial complex</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-DDEFarewell_249-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DDEFarewell-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>He elaborated, "we recognize the imperative need for this development&#160;... the potential for the disastrous rise of misplaced power exists and will persist&#160;... Only an alert and knowledgeable citizenry can compel the proper meshing of the huge industrial and military machinery of defense with our peaceful methods and goals, so that security and liberty may prosper together."<sup id="cite_ref-DDEFarewell_249-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DDEFarewell-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of legal issues related to holding a military rank while in a civilian office, Eisenhower had resigned his permanent commission as <a href="/wiki/General_of_the_Army_(United_States)" title="General of the Army (United States)">General of the Army</a> before entering the office of President of the United States. Upon completion of his Presidential term, his commission was reactivated by Congress and Eisenhower again was commissioned a five-star general in the United States Army.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Post-presidency.2C_death_and_funeral"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-presidency,_death_and_funeral">Post-presidency, death and funeral</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dwight_Eisenhower_at_1964_RNC_(cropped1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Dwight_Eisenhower_at_1964_RNC_%28cropped1%29.jpg/220px-Dwight_Eisenhower_at_1964_RNC_%28cropped1%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Dwight_Eisenhower_at_1964_RNC_%28cropped1%29.jpg/330px-Dwight_Eisenhower_at_1964_RNC_%28cropped1%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Dwight_Eisenhower_at_1964_RNC_%28cropped1%29.jpg/440px-Dwight_Eisenhower_at_1964_RNC_%28cropped1%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2315" data-file-height="1966" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dwight_Eisenhower_at_1964_RNC_(cropped1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eisenhower speaks to the press at the <a href="/wiki/1964_Republican_National_Convention" title="1964 Republican National Convention">1964 Republican National Convention</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LBJ_and_Eisenhower.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/LBJ_and_Eisenhower.jpg/220px-LBJ_and_Eisenhower.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/LBJ_and_Eisenhower.jpg/330px-LBJ_and_Eisenhower.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/LBJ_and_Eisenhower.jpg/440px-LBJ_and_Eisenhower.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1364" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LBJ_and_Eisenhower.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson" title="Lyndon B. Johnson">President Lyndon Johnson</a> with Eisenhower aboard <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_One" title="Air Force One">Air Force One</a> in October 1965</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Funeral_services_for_Dwight_D._Eisenhower,_March_1969.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Funeral_services_for_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_March_1969.jpg/220px-Funeral_services_for_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_March_1969.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Funeral_services_for_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_March_1969.jpg/330px-Funeral_services_for_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_March_1969.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Funeral_services_for_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_March_1969.jpg/440px-Funeral_services_for_Dwight_D._Eisenhower%2C_March_1969.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1926" data-file-height="1803" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Funeral_services_for_Dwight_D._Eisenhower,_March_1969.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eisenhower's funeral service</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DDEisenhowerGrave3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/DDEisenhowerGrave3.jpg/220px-DDEisenhowerGrave3.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/DDEisenhowerGrave3.jpg/330px-DDEisenhowerGrave3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/DDEisenhowerGrave3.jpg/440px-DDEisenhowerGrave3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3072" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DDEisenhowerGrave3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graves of Dwight D. Eisenhower, Doud Dwight "Icky" Eisenhower and Mamie Eisenhower in Abilene, Kansas</div></div></div>
<p>Following the presidency, Eisenhower moved to the place where he and Mamie had spent much of their post-war time. The home was a working farm adjacent to the battlefield at <a href="/wiki/Gettysburg,_Pennsylvania" title="Gettysburg, Pennsylvania">Gettysburg, Pennsylvania</a>, 70 miles from his ancestral home in Elizabethville, Dauphin County, Pennsylvania.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> They also maintained a retirement home in <a href="/wiki/Palm_Desert,_California" title="Palm Desert, California">Palm Desert, California</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> In 1967 the Eisenhowers donated the Gettysburg farm to the <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">National Park Service</a>.
</p><p>After leaving office, Eisenhower did not completely retreat from political life. He flew to San Antonio, where he had been stationed years earlier, to support <a href="/wiki/John_W._Goode" title="John W. Goode">John W. Goode</a>, the unsuccessful Republican candidate against the Democrat <a href="/wiki/Henry_B._Gonzalez" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry B. Gonzalez">Henry B. Gonzalez</a> for <a href="/wiki/Texas%27_20th_congressional_district" class="mw-redirect" title="Texas&#39; 20th congressional district">Texas' 20th congressional district</a> seat.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> He addressed the <a href="/wiki/1964_Republican_National_Convention" title="1964 Republican National Convention">1964 Republican National Convention</a>, in San Francisco, and appeared with party nominee <a href="/wiki/Barry_Goldwater" title="Barry Goldwater">Barry Goldwater</a> in a campaign commercial from his Gettysburg retreat.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> That endorsement came somewhat reluctantly because Goldwater had in the late 1950s criticized Eisenhower's administration as "a dime-store New Deal".<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> On January 20, 1969, <a href="/wiki/First_inauguration_of_Richard_Nixon" title="First inauguration of Richard Nixon">the day Nixon was inaugurated as President</a>, Eisenhower issued a statement praising his former vice president and calling it a "day for rejoicing".<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><span id="Death"></span>
On the morning of March 28, 1969, Eisenhower died in Washington, D.C., of <a href="/wiki/Congestive_heart_failure" class="mw-redirect" title="Congestive heart failure">congestive heart failure</a> at <a href="/wiki/Walter_Reed_Army_Medical_Center" title="Walter Reed Army Medical Center">Walter Reed Army Medical Center</a>; he was 78 years old. The following day, his body was moved to the <a href="/wiki/Washington_National_Cathedral" title="Washington National Cathedral">Washington National Cathedral</a>'s Bethlehem Chapel, where he lay in repose for 28 hours.<sup id="cite_ref-FinalPost_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FinalPost-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> He was then transported to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">United States Capitol</a>, where he <a href="/wiki/Lying_in_state#United_States" title="Lying in state">lay in state</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Capitol_Rotunda" class="mw-redirect" title="Capitol Rotunda">Capitol Rotunda</a> March 30–31.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/State_funeral" title="State funeral">state funeral</a> service was conducted at the Washington National Cathedral on March 31. The president and First Lady, Richard and Pat Nixon, attended, as did former president Lyndon Johnson. Also among the 2,000 invited guests were several <a href="/wiki/Heads_of_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Heads of state">Heads of state</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a> of France and the <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Reza_Pahlavi" title="Mohammad Reza Pahlavi">Shah of Iran</a>. The service included the singing of <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Faure" title="Jean-Baptiste Faure">Faure</a>'s <i>The Palms</i>, and the playing of <a href="/wiki/Onward,_Christian_Soldiers" title="Onward, Christian Soldiers">Onward, Christian Soldiers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>That evening, Eisenhower's body was placed onto a special <a href="/wiki/Funeral_train" title="Funeral train">funeral train</a> for its journey from the nation's capital through seven states to his hometown of <a href="/wiki/Abilene,_Kansas" title="Abilene, Kansas">Abilene, Kansas</a>. First incorporated into President <a href="/wiki/Funeral_and_burial_of_Abraham_Lincoln" title="Funeral and burial of Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln's funeral</a> in 1865, a funeral train would not be part of a U.S. state funeral again until <a href="/wiki/Death_and_state_funeral_of_George_H._W._Bush" title="Death and state funeral of George H. W. Bush">2018</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> Eisenhower is buried inside the Place of Meditation, the chapel on the grounds of the <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower_Presidential_Library,_Museum_and_Boyhood_Home" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower Presidential Library, Museum and Boyhood Home">Eisenhower Presidential Center</a> in Abeline. As requested, he was buried in a <a href="/wiki/G.I._(military)" title="G.I. (military)">Government Issue</a> casket, and wearing his <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_jacket" title="Eisenhower jacket">World War II uniform</a>, decorated with: Army Distinguished Service Medal with three oak leaf clusters, Navy Distinguished Service Medal, and the Legion of Merit. Buried alongside Eisenhower are his son Doud, who died at age 3 in 1921, and wife Mamie, who died in 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-FinalPost_259-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FinalPost-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>President Richard Nixon eulogized Eisenhower in 1969, saying:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Some men are considered great because they lead great armies or they lead powerful nations. For eight years now, Dwight Eisenhower has neither commanded an army nor led a nation; and yet he remained through his final days the world's most admired and respected man, truly the first citizen of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_memory">Legacy and memory</span></h2>
<p>Eisenhower's reputation declined in the immediate years after he left office. During his presidency, he was widely seen by critics as an inactive, uninspiring, golf-playing president. This was in stark contrast to his vigorous young successor, <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>, who was 26 years his junior. Despite his unprecedented use of Army troops to enforce a federal desegregation order at <a href="/wiki/Little_Rock_Central_High_School" title="Little Rock Central High School">Central High School</a> in Little Rock, Eisenhower was criticized for his reluctance to support the <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement">civil rights movement</a> to the degree that activists wanted. Eisenhower also attracted criticism for his handling of the <a href="/wiki/1960_U-2_incident" title="1960 U-2 incident">1960 U-2 incident</a> and the associated international embarrassment,<sup id="cite_ref-&#39;70s_27_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-&#39;70s_27-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-us_news_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-us_news-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> for the Soviet Union's perceived leadership in the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_arms_race" title="Nuclear arms race">nuclear arms race</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a>, and for his failure to publicly oppose <a href="/wiki/McCarthyism" title="McCarthyism">McCarthyism</a>.
</p><p>In particular, Eisenhower was criticized for failing to defend <a href="/wiki/George_Marshall" title="George Marshall">George Marshall</a> from attacks by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_McCarthy" title="Joseph McCarthy">Joseph McCarthy</a>, though he privately deplored McCarthy's tactics and claims.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historian <a href="/wiki/John_Lewis_Gaddis" title="John Lewis Gaddis">John Lewis Gaddis</a> has summarized a more recent turnaround in evaluations by historians:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Historians long ago abandoned the view that Eisenhower's was a failed presidency. He did, after all, end the Korean War without getting into any others. He stabilized, and did not escalate, the Soviet–American rivalry. He strengthened European alliances while withdrawing support from European colonialism. He rescued the Republican Party from isolationism and McCarthyism. He maintained prosperity, balanced the budget, promoted technological innovation, facilitated (if reluctantly) the civil rights movement and warned, in the most memorable farewell address since Washington's, of a "<a href="/wiki/Military%E2%80%93industrial_complex" title="Military–industrial complex">military–industrial complex</a>" that could endanger the nation's liberties. Not until Reagan would another president leave office with so strong a sense of having accomplished what he set out to do.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:72-901-1_HR7786_Veterans_Day_June_1_1954.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/72-901-1_HR7786_Veterans_Day_June_1_1954.jpg/240px-72-901-1_HR7786_Veterans_Day_June_1_1954.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="191" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/72-901-1_HR7786_Veterans_Day_June_1_1954.jpg/360px-72-901-1_HR7786_Veterans_Day_June_1_1954.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/72-901-1_HR7786_Veterans_Day_June_1_1954.jpg/480px-72-901-1_HR7786_Veterans_Day_June_1_1954.jpg 2x" data-file-width="504" data-file-height="401" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:72-901-1_HR7786_Veterans_Day_June_1_1954.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eisenhower signs the legislation that changes <a href="/wiki/Armistice_Day" title="Armistice Day">Armistice Day</a> to <a href="/wiki/Veterans_Day_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Veterans Day (United States)">Veterans Day</a>, June 1, 1954</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Meeting_with_President_Eisenhower._President_Kennedy,_President_Eisenhower,_military_aides._Camp_David,_MD._-_NARA_-_194198.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Meeting_with_President_Eisenhower._President_Kennedy%2C_President_Eisenhower%2C_military_aides._Camp_David%2C_MD._-_NARA_-_194198.jpg/240px-Meeting_with_President_Eisenhower._President_Kennedy%2C_President_Eisenhower%2C_military_aides._Camp_David%2C_MD._-_NARA_-_194198.jpg" decoding="async" width="240" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Meeting_with_President_Eisenhower._President_Kennedy%2C_President_Eisenhower%2C_military_aides._Camp_David%2C_MD._-_NARA_-_194198.jpg/360px-Meeting_with_President_Eisenhower._President_Kennedy%2C_President_Eisenhower%2C_military_aides._Camp_David%2C_MD._-_NARA_-_194198.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Meeting_with_President_Eisenhower._President_Kennedy%2C_President_Eisenhower%2C_military_aides._Camp_David%2C_MD._-_NARA_-_194198.jpg/480px-Meeting_with_President_Eisenhower._President_Kennedy%2C_President_Eisenhower%2C_military_aides._Camp_David%2C_MD._-_NARA_-_194198.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2966" data-file-height="2342" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Meeting_with_President_Eisenhower._President_Kennedy,_President_Eisenhower,_military_aides._Camp_David,_MD._-_NARA_-_194198.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> meets with Eisenhower at <a href="/wiki/Camp_David" title="Camp David">Camp David</a>, April 22, 1961, three days after the failed <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Pigs_Invasion" title="Bay of Pigs Invasion">Bay of Pigs Invasion</a></div></div></div>
<p>Although conservatism in politics was strong during the 1950s, and Eisenhower generally espoused conservative sentiments, his administration concerned itself mostly with foreign affairs (an area in which the career-military president had more knowledge) and pursued a hands-off domestic policy. Eisenhower looked to moderation and cooperation as a means of governance.<sup id="cite_ref-jstor.org_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jstor.org-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although he sought to slow or contain the <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a> and other federal programs, he did not attempt to repeal them outright. In doing so, Eisenhower was popular among the liberal wing of the Republican Party.<sup id="cite_ref-jstor.org_268-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jstor.org-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> Conservative critics of his administration thought that he did not do enough to advance the goals of the right; according to <a href="/wiki/Hans_Morgenthau" title="Hans Morgenthau">Hans Morgenthau</a>, "Eisenhower's victories were but accidents without consequence in the history of the Republican party."<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since the 19th century, many if not all presidents were assisted by a central figure or "gatekeeper", sometimes described as the president's private secretary, sometimes with no official title at all.<sup id="cite_ref-medved_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-medved-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> Eisenhower formalized this role, introducing the office of <a href="/wiki/White_House_Chief_of_Staff" title="White House Chief of Staff">White House Chief of Staff</a>&#160;– an idea he borrowed from the United States Army. Every president after <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_Johnson" class="mw-redirect" title="Lyndon Johnson">Lyndon Johnson</a> has also appointed staff to this position. Initially, <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a> tried to operate without a chief of staff, but each eventually appointed one.
</p><p>As president, Eisenhower also initiated the "<a href="/wiki/Up_or_out" title="Up or out">up or out</a>" policy that still prevails in the U.S. military. Officers who are passed over for promotion twice, are then usually honorably but quickly discharged, in order to make way for younger, and more able officers. (As an army officer, Eisenhower had been stuck at the rank of major for 16 years in the <a href="/wiki/Interwar_period" title="Interwar period">interwar period</a>.)
</p><p>On December 20, 1944, Eisenhower was appointed to the rank of <a href="/wiki/General_of_the_Army_(United_States)" title="General of the Army (United States)">General of the Army</a>, placing him in the company of <a href="/wiki/George_Marshall" title="George Marshall">George Marshall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_H._Arnold" title="Henry H. Arnold">Henry "Hap" Arnold</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Douglas_MacArthur" title="Douglas MacArthur">Douglas MacArthur</a>, the only four men to achieve the rank in World War II. Along with <a href="/wiki/Omar_Bradley" title="Omar Bradley">Omar Bradley</a>, they were the only five men to achieve the rank since the August 5, 1888 death of <a href="/wiki/Philip_Sheridan" title="Philip Sheridan">Philip Sheridan</a>, and the only five men to hold the rank of <a href="/wiki/Five-star_rank" title="Five-star rank">five-star general</a>. The rank was created by an <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Act of Congress</a> on a temporary basis, when <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Public Law</a> <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Public_Law_78-482" class="extiw" title="s:Public Law 78-482">78-482</a> was passed on December 14, 1944,<sup id="cite_ref-PL78-482_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PL78-482-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup> as a temporary rank, subject to reversion to permanent rank six months after the end of the war. The temporary rank was then declared permanent on March 23, 1946 by Public Law 333 of the <a href="/wiki/79th_Congress" class="mw-redirect" title="79th Congress">79th Congress</a>, which also awarded full pay and allowances in the grade to those on the retired list.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> It was created to give the most senior American commanders parity of rank with their British counterparts holding the ranks of <a href="/wiki/Field_marshal_(United_Kingdom)" title="Field marshal (United Kingdom)">field marshal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Admiral_of_the_fleet_(Royal_Navy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Admiral of the fleet (Royal Navy)">admiral of the fleet</a>. This second General of the Army rank is not the same as the post-Civil War era version because of its purpose and five stars.
</p>

1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Dwight_D._Eisenhower_POTUS_Appreciation_Medal_Hawaii_Reverse.jpg/240px-Dwight_D._Eisenhower_POTUS_Appreciation_Medal_Hawaii_Reverse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="610" data-file-height="610" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><a href="/wiki/Frank_Gasparro" title="Frank Gasparro">Frank Gasparro</a>'s obverse design (left) and reverse design (right) of the Presidential Medal of Appreciation award during Eisenhower's official visit to the State of Hawaii from June 20–25, 1960</div></div></div></div>
<p>Eisenhower founded <a href="/wiki/People_to_People_International" title="People to People International">People to People International</a> in 1956, based on his belief that citizen interaction would promote cultural interaction and <a href="/wiki/World_peace" title="World peace">world peace</a>. The program includes a <a href="/wiki/People_to_People_Student_Ambassadors" class="mw-redirect" title="People to People Student Ambassadors">student ambassador component</a>, which sends American youth on educational trips to other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During his second term as president, Eisenhower distinctively preserved his presidential gratitude by awarding individuals a special memento. This memento was a series of specially designed U.S. Mint presidential appreciation medals. Eisenhower presented the medal as an expression of his appreciation and the medal is a keepsake reminder for the recipient.<sup id="cite_ref-Gomez_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gomez-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The development of the appreciation medals was initiated by the White House and executed by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Mint" title="United States Mint">United States Mint</a>, through the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Mint" title="Philadelphia Mint">Philadelphia Mint</a>. The medals were struck from September 1958 through October 1960. A total of twenty designs are cataloged with a total mintage of 9,858. Each of the designs incorporates the text "with appreciation" or "with personal and official gratitude" accompanied with Eisenhower's initials "D.D.E." or facsimile signature. The design also incorporates location, date, and/or significant event. Prior to the end of his second term as President, 1,451 medals were turned in to the Bureau of the Mint and destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-Gomez_275-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gomez-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> The Eisenhower appreciation medals are part of the Presidential Medal of Appreciation Award Medal Series.<sup id="cite_ref-Gomez_275-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gomez-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tributes_and_memorials">Tributes and memorials</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_memorials_to_Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="List of memorials to Dwight D. Eisenhower">List of memorials to Dwight D. Eisenhower</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenhower_Interstate_System_IMG_4192.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Eisenhower_Interstate_System_IMG_4192.JPG/250px-Eisenhower_Interstate_System_IMG_4192.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Eisenhower_Interstate_System_IMG_4192.JPG/375px-Eisenhower_Interstate_System_IMG_4192.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Eisenhower_Interstate_System_IMG_4192.JPG/500px-Eisenhower_Interstate_System_IMG_4192.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenhower_Interstate_System_IMG_4192.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eisenhower Interstate System sign south of <a href="/wiki/San_Antonio" title="San Antonio">San Antonio</a>, Texas</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenhower_bronze.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Eisenhower_bronze.jpg/170px-Eisenhower_bronze.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="340" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/29/Eisenhower_bronze.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eisenhower_bronze.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower_(Brothers)" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower (Brothers)">Bronze statue of Eisenhower</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol_rotunda" title="United States Capitol rotunda">Capitol rotunda</a><sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The Interstate Highway System is officially known as the "Dwight D. Eisenhower National System of Interstate and Defense Highways" in his honor. It was inspired in part by Eisenhower's own Army experiences in World War II, where he recognized the advantages of the <a href="/wiki/Autobahn" title="Autobahn">autobahn</a> system in Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-archivesIHS_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archivesIHS-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Commemorative signs reading "Eisenhower Interstate System" and bearing Eisenhower's permanent <a href="/wiki/General_of_the_Army_(United_States)" title="General of the Army (United States)">5-star rank</a> insignia were introduced in 1993 and now are displayed throughout the Interstate System. Several highways are also named for him, including the <a href="/wiki/Interstate_290_(Illinois)" title="Interstate 290 (Illinois)">Eisenhower Expressway</a> (Interstate 290) near <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>. the <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_Tunnel" title="Eisenhower Tunnel">Eisenhower Tunnel</a> on <a href="/wiki/Interstate_70" title="Interstate 70">Interstate 70</a> west of <a href="/wiki/Denver" title="Denver">Denver</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Interstate_80_in_California" title="Interstate 80 in California">Interstate 80 in California</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower_School_for_National_Security_and_Resource_Strategy" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower School for National Security and Resource Strategy">Dwight D. Eisenhower School for National Security and Resource Strategy</a> is a senior war college of the Department of Defense's <a href="/wiki/National_Defense_University" title="National Defense University">National Defense University</a> in Washington, DC. Eisenhower graduated from this school when it was previously known as the <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower_School_for_National_Security_and_Resource_Strategy" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower School for National Security and Resource Strategy">Army Industrial College</a>. The school's building on <a href="/wiki/Fort_Lesley_J._McNair" title="Fort Lesley J. McNair">Fort Lesley J. McNair</a>, when it was known as the Industrial College of the Armed Forces, was dedicated as Eisenhower Hall in 1960.
</p><p>Eisenhower was honored on a <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_dollar" title="Eisenhower dollar">US one dollar coin</a>, minted from 1971 to 1978. His centenary was honored on a <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_commemorative_dollar" class="mw-redirect" title="Eisenhower commemorative dollar">commemorative dollar coin</a> issued in 1990.
</p><p>In 1969 four major record companies&#160;– <a href="/wiki/ABC_Records" title="ABC Records">ABC Records</a>, <a href="/wiki/MGM_Records" title="MGM Records">MGM Records</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddha_Records" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddha Records">Buddha Records</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caedmon_Audio" title="Caedmon Audio">Caedmon Audio</a>&#160;– released tribute albums in Eisenhower's honor.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1999 the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> created the <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower_Memorial" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower Memorial">Dwight D. Eisenhower Memorial</a> Commission, to create an enduring <a href="/wiki/National_memorial" class="mw-redirect" title="National memorial">national memorial</a> in Washington, D.C.. In 2009 the commission chose the architect <a href="/wiki/Frank_Gehry" title="Frank Gehry">Frank Gehry</a> to design the memorial.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WPmemorial_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WPmemorial-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> The memorial will stand on a four-acre site near the <a href="/wiki/National_Mall" title="National Mall">National Mall</a> on Maryland Avenue, SW across the street from the <a href="/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum" title="National Air and Space Museum">National Air and Space Museum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 1999 he was listed on <a href="/wiki/Gallup%27s_List_of_Widely_Admired_People" class="mw-redirect" title="Gallup&#39;s List of Widely Admired People">Gallup's List of Most Widely Admired People of the 20th century</a>. In 2009 he was named to the <a href="/wiki/World_Golf_Hall_of_Fame" title="World Golf Hall of Fame">World Golf Hall of Fame</a> in the Lifetime Achievement category for his contributions to the sport.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<p>Note&#160;– Eisenhower relinquished his active duty status when he became president on January 20, 1953. He was returned to active duty when he left office eight years later.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-74"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-74">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Eisenhower lived in 'Telegraph Cottage', Warren Road, <a href="/wiki/Coombe,_Kingston_upon_Thames" title="Coombe, Kingston upon Thames">Coombe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingston_upon_Thames" title="Kingston upon Thames">Kingston upon Thames</a> from 1942 to 1944. In 1995, a plaque commemorating this was placed there by the Royal Borough of Kingston upon Thames. It can be seen at the north end of Warren Road.</span>

<li id="cite_note-215"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-215">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.press.uchicago.edu/Misc/Chicago/404811in.html">"An interview with David K. Johnson author of The Lavender Scare: The Cold War Persecution of Gays and Lesbians in the Federal Government"</a>. <i>press.uchicago.edu</i>. The University of Chicago. 2004.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=press.uchicago.edu&amp;rft.atitle=An+interview+with+David+K.+Johnson+author+of+The+Lavender+Scare%3A+The+Cold+War+Persecution+of+Gays+and+Lesbians+in+the+Federal+Government&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.press.uchicago.edu%2FMisc%2FChicago%2F404811in.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADwight+D.+Eisenhower" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-216"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-216">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Adkins, Judith. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.archives.gov/publications/prologue/2016/summer/lavender.html">"<span class="cs1-kern-left">'</span>These People Are Frightened to Death' Congressional Investigations and the Lavender Scare"</a>. <i>archives.gov</i>. The U.S. National Archives and Records Administration. <q>Most significantly, the 1950 congressional investigations and the Hoey committee's final report helped institutionalize discrimination by laying the groundwork for President Dwight D. Eisenhower's 1953 Executive Order #10450, 'Security Requirements for Government Employment.' That order explicitly added sexuality to the criteria used to determine suitability for federal employment.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=archives.gov&amp;rft.atitle=%27These+People+Are+Frightened+to+Death%27+Congressional+Investigations+and+the+Lavender+Scare&amp;rft.aulast=Adkins&amp;rft.aufirst=Judith&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.archives.gov%2Fpublications%2Fprologue%2F2016%2Fsummer%2Flavender.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADwight+D.+Eisenhower" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-documenting-217"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-documenting_217-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-documenting_217-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Sears, Brad; Hunter, Nan D.; Mallory, Christy (September 2009). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://williamsinstitute.law.ucla.edu/wp-content/uploads/5_History.pdf#page=3"><i>Documenting Discrimination on the Basis of Sexual Orientation and Gender Identity in State Employment</i></a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. Los Angeles: The Williams Institute on Sexual Orientation and Gender Identity Law and Public Policy at the University of California Los Angeles School of Law. pp.&#160;5–3. <q>From 1947 to 1961, more than 5,000 allegedly homosexual federal civil servants lost their jobs in the purges for no reason other than sexual orientation, and thousands of applicants were also rejected for federal employment for the same reason. During this period, more than 1,000 men and women were fired for suspected homosexuality from the State Department alone—a far greater number than were dismissed for their membership in the Communist party.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Documenting+Discrimination+on+the+Basis+of+Sexual+Orientation+and+Gender+Identity+in+State+Employment&amp;rft.place=Los+Angeles&amp;rft.pages=5-3&amp;rft.pub=The+Williams+Institute+on+Sexual+Orientation+and+Gender+Identity+Law+and+Public+Policy+at+the+University+of+California+Los+Angeles+School+of+Law&amp;rft.date=2009-09&amp;rft.aulast=Sears&amp;rft.aufirst=Brad&amp;rft.au=Hunter%2C+Nan+D.&amp;rft.au=Mallory%2C+Christy&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwilliamsinstitute.law.ucla.edu%2Fwp-content%2Fuploads%2F5_History.pdf%23page%3D3&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADwight+D.+Eisenhower" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-218"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-218">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Adkins, Judith. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.archives.gov/publications/prologue/2016/summer/lavender.html">"<span class="cs1-kern-left">'</span>These People Are Frightened to Death' Congressional Investigations and the Lavender Scare"</a>. <i>archives.gov</i>. The U.S. National Archives and Records Administration. <q>Historians estimate that somewhere between 5,000 and tens of thousands of gay workers lost their jobs during the Lavender Scare.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=archives.gov&amp;rft.atitle=%27These+People+Are+Frightened+to+Death%27+Congressional+Investigations+and+the+Lavender+Scare&amp;rft.aulast=Adkins&amp;rft.aufirst=Judith&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.archives.gov%2Fpublications%2Fprologue%2F2016%2Fsummer%2Flavender.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADwight+D.+Eisenhower" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-219"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-219">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Sears, Brad; Hunter, Nan D.; Mallory, Christy (September 2009). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://williamsinstitute.law.ucla.edu/wp-content/uploads/5_History.pdf#page=3"><i>Documenting Discrimination on the Basis of Sexual Orientation and Gender Identity in State Employment</i></a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. Los Angeles: The Williams Institute on Sexual Orientation and Gender Identity Law and Public Policy at the University of California Los Angeles School of Law. pp.&#160;5–3. <q>Johnson has demonstrated that during this era government officials intentionally engaged in campaigns to associate homosexuality with Communism: 'homosexual' and 'pervert' became synonyms for 'Communist' and 'traitor.'</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Documenting+Discrimination+on+the+Basis+of+Sexual+Orientation+and+Gender+Identity+in+State+Employment&amp;rft.place=Los+Angeles&amp;rft.pages=5-3&amp;rft.pub=The+Williams+Institute+on+Sexual+Orientation+and+Gender+Identity+Law+and+Public+Policy+at+the+University+of+California+Los+Angeles+School+of+Law&amp;rft.date=2009-09&amp;rft.aulast=Sears&amp;rft.aufirst=Brad&amp;rft.au=Hunter%2C+Nan+D.&amp;rft.au=Mallory%2C+Christy&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwilliamsinstitute.law.ucla.edu%2Fwp-content%2Fuploads%2F5_History.pdf%23page%3D3&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADwight+D.+Eisenhower" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-240"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-240">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news">Leviero, Anthony (June 9, 1956). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://search.proquest.com/docview/113808030/">"President Undergoes Surgery on Intestine Block at 2:59 A.M.: Doctors Pronounce It Success&#160;: Condition Is Good: Operation Lasts Hour and 53 Minutes–13 Attend Him"</a>. <i>The New York Times</i>. p.&#160;1. <q>President Eisenhower was operated on at 2:59 A.M. today for relief of an intestinal obstruction. At 4:55 A.M., the operation was pronounced a success by the surgeons. ... The President's condition was diagnosed as ileitis. This is an inflamation of the ileum—the lowest portion of the small intestine, where it joins the large intestine. ... The President first felt ill shortly after midnight yesterday. He had attended a dinner of the White House News Photographers Association Thursday night and had returned to the White House at 11. Mrs. Eisenhower called Maj. Gen. Howard McC. Snyder, the President's personal physician, at 12:45 A.M. yesterday, telling him the President had some discomfort in his stomach. He recommended a slight dose of milk of magnesia. At 1:20 Mrs. Eisenhower called again, saying the President was still complaining of not feeling well. This time she asked Dr. Snyder to come to the White House from his home about a mile away on Connecticut Avenue. He arrived at 2 A.M. and has not left the President's side since.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+New+York+Times&amp;rft.atitle=President+Undergoes+Surgery+on+Intestine+Block+at+2%3A59+A.M.%3A+Doctors+Pronounce+It+Success+%3A+Condition+Is+Good%3A+Operation+Lasts+Hour+and+53+Minutes%E2%80%9313+Attend+Him&amp;rft.pages=1&amp;rft.date=1956-06-09&amp;rft.aulast=Leviero&amp;rft.aufirst=Anthony&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fsearch.proquest.com%2Fdocview%2F113808030%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADwight+D.+Eisenhower" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
